The double precision didn't change anything — same exact error. This confirms the issue is NOT in the online softmax accumulation. The first 10 dims match exactly between ref and sol, but dim 61 is completely different (ref=0.043 sol=-0.051, diff=0.094). This pattern (some dims exact match, some wildly different) is characteristic of a bug in which tokens contribute to the output. If the softmax weights are slightly different (due to numerical precision), and the V values differ significantly between the tokens whose weights changed, the output could flip. But we already verified that the dot products match the reference exactly (to 1e-8). So the softmax weights should be the same (with or without online softmax, which is mathematically equivalent). Unless there's a bug I'm not seeing. Wait, let me check: when I ran the NumPy warp-shuffle simulation, I used: ```python for w in range(4): w_partial = partial[w*32:(w+1)*32].copy() for off in [16, 8, 4, 2, 1]: for i in range(32 - off): if i + off < len(w_partial): w_partial[i] += w_partial[i + off] warp_sums[w] = w_partial[0] ``` This simulates the warp shuffle reduction (within each warp, then cross-warp sum). But in the ACTUAL CUDA kernel, the warp shuffle uses `__shfl_down_sync` which shifts data DOWN (from higher lanes to lower lanes). My Python simulation also shifts data DOWN (element i gets element i+off's value). Actually wait, in CUDA's `__shfl_down_sync`: - Lane i gets the value from lane i + delta - If i + delta >= warpSize, lane i gets its own value So for offset=16: lane 0 gets lane 16's value, lane 16 gets its own value. For offset=8: lane 0 gets lane 8's value (which now holds lane 8 + lane 24, after the previous iteration). The reduction tree with `__shfl_down_sync` is: ``` After off=16: lanes 0..15 have a[i] + a[i+16], lanes 16..31 unchanged After off=8: lanes 0..7 have (a[i]+a[i+16]) + (a[i+8]+a[i+24]), etc. ... After off=1: lane 0 has sum of all ``` My Python simulation: ```python for off in [16, 8, 4, 2, 1]: for i in range(32 - off): if i + off < len(w_partial): w_partial[i] += w_partial[i + off] ``` This does: w_partial[i] += w_partial[i + off]. For off=16: - i=0: w_partial[0] += w_partial[16] - i=1: w_partial[1] += w_partial[17] - ... - i=15: w_partial[15] += w_partial[31] This is the same as `__shfl_down_sync` for lanes with valid data. For i>=16, there are no i+off < 32, so nothing happens (they keep their own values). This matches `__shfl_down_sync` where lane i gets its own value if i+off >= warpSize. So the reduction is IDENTICAL. After cross-warp sum, the final score is the sum of 4 warp-sums. This uses a simple sequential sum. My Python simulation also uses sequential sum. The overall reduction order should be EXACTLY the same. And the scores match to 1e-8 as verified. So the scores are the same. Which means the softmax should produce the SAME result (mathematically). But the kernel output differs by 0.094. So the bug MUST be in how the kernel uses the scores for softmax → accumulation. Let me re-check the online softmax logic VERY carefully. Hmm, wait. Let me check if the online softmax is correctly computing the softmax weights. In my kernel, for each page: 1. Compute scores (correct) 2. Find page max (correct) 3. Update global max: new_max = max(running_max, page_max) (correct) 4. Rescale: rescale = exp(running_max - new_max) (correct for softening old contributions) 5. Compute softmax numerators: prob = exp(score - new_max) (correct) 6. Update running_sum with new contributions (correct) 7. Accumulate output (correct) But wait — there's a subtle issue with step 4. The running_sum was accumulated using the OLD max. When we rescale, we do: ``` running_sum *= exp(old_max - new_max) ``` This rescales each OLD contribution s_i = exp(score_i - old_max) to s_i' = exp(score_i - old_max + old_max - new_max) = exp(score_i - new_max). This is mathematically correct. But the running_sum is a SINGLE NUMBER — the sum of all old exp contributions. Rescaling this single number by exp(old_max - new_max) is correct because: ``` sum_i [exp(score_i - old_max)] * exp(old_max - new_max) = sum_i [exp(score_i - old_max + old_max - new_max)] = sum_i [exp(score_i - new_max)] ``` This is mathematically valid. And with double precision, the rescaling should be very accurate. Similarly for acc: ``` acc *= exp(old_max - new_max) ``` Where acc = sum_i [exp(score_i - old_max) * V_i]. After rescaling: ``` acc' = sum_i [exp(score_i - new_max) * V_i] ``` This is also mathematically correct. So WHERE is the bug? Let me check one more thing: is thread 0 REALLY the only one that has the correct max after the warp shuffle? In my max reduction: ```cuda float page_max = -1e30f; if (tid < tok_this_page) { page_max = scratch[tid]; // score } // Warp reduce max for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, page_max, off); page_max = fmaxf(page_max, other); } // Broadcast page_max = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, page_max, 0); ``` After the reduction, lane 0 has the max of all lanes 0..31. Lanes >= tok_this_page had page_max = -1e30f which doesn't affect the max. The broadcast copies lane 0's value to all lanes. But does lane 0 have the correct value? Let me trace: with `tok_this_page = 5` (say): - Lanes 0..4 have valid scores - Lanes 5..31 have -1e30f After off=16: - Lane 0 gets max(score[0], -1e30f) = score[0] (via shuffle from lane 16) - Lanes 1..4 get their own values (via shuffle from lanes 17..20) - Wait, `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, page_max, 16)`: - Lane 0 gets lane 16's page_max = -1e30f - Lane 1 gets lane 17's page_max = -1e30f - ... - Lane 15 gets lane 31's page_max = -1e30f - Lanes 16..31 get their own values (since 16+16 > 31) - After: lanes 0..4 have max(score[0..4], -1e30f) = score[0..4] - Lanes 5..31 have -1e30f After off=8: - Lane 0 gets max(score[0], lane 8) = max(score[0], -1e30f) = score[0] - Lanes 1..4 get score[1..4] (their own) - ... After off=4: - Lane 0 gets max(score[0], lane 4) = max(score[0], score[4]) - ... After off=2: - Lane 0 gets max(max(score[0], score[4]), lane 2) = max(score[0], score[4], score[2]) After off=1: - Lane 0 gets max(max(...), lane 1) = max(score[0], score[4], score[2], score[1]) So lane 0 has max(score[0], score[2], score[4], score[1]). It's missing score[3]! The warp shuffle reduction pattern with `__shfl_down_sync` ONLY works if all lanes participate (i.e., all have valid data). When only a SUBSET of lanes have valid data, the reduction is INCOMPLETE because some lanes never get included in the reduction tree. THIS IS THE BUG! The wag down-shuffle reduction requires that ALL lanes participate. If lanes 5..31 have dummy values (-1e30f for max, 0 for sum), the reduction still works because dummy values don't affect the result. import sys; sys.path.insert(0, '.')

sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).cuda().eval()

total_pages = 8

kv_cache[3, 0, 0, 0] = 1.0    # K
kv_cache[3, 0, 0, D] = 2.0    # V
# Another token: block 5, position 0, kv_head 0, dim 0: K=0.5, V=0.5
kv_cache[5, 0, 0, 0] = 0.5    # K
kv_cache[5, 0, 0, D] = 0.5    # V

seq_lens = torch.tensor([2], dtype=torch.int32, device='cuda')

print(f'Max diff: {diff:.10f}')
print(f'Match: {torch.allclose(ref_out, sol_out, atol=0.02, rtol=0.02)}')

import torch, math
import reference, solution

# Test with seq_len that spans multiple pages, non-trivial GQA, and scaling
for seq_len in [16, 32, 64, 128]:

        reference.HEAD_DIM = D; reference.SEQ_LEN = seq_len; reference.PAGE_SIZE = P

But since there's no explicit barrier between the grid-stride loop and the second pass... actually, the grid-stride loop and the warp reduction use the same threads on the same threadblock, so there's no concurrency issue. Each thread processes its assigned elements in the grid-stride loop, then participates in the warp reduction, then syncs, then starts the second pass. Since all threads in the block participate, and the grid-stride loop writes to non-overlapping indices, there's no race. import sys; sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference, solution

# Test the worst-case scenario from earlier: seq_len=32, scale=8, D=64, Hkv=2
all_ok = True
for seq_len in [16, 32, 64, 128, 256]:
    for scale in [1.0, 8.0]:
        B = 1; H = 4; Hkv = 2; D = 64; P = 16
        reference.BATCH = B; reference.NUM_HEADS = H; reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = Hkv
        reference.HEAD_DIM = D; reference.SEQ_LEN = seq_len; reference.PAGE_SIZE = P

        init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
        ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).cuda().eval()
        sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).cuda().eval()
        sol_model.load_state_dict(ref_model.state_dict(), strict=True)

        for seed in range(5):
            torch.manual_seed(seed); torch.cuda.manual_seed_all(seed)
            inputs = [t.cuda() for t in reference.get_inputs()]
            inputs[0] = inputs[0] * scale
            inputs[1] = inputs[1] * scale

            with torch.no_grad():
                ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
                sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)

            ok = torch.allclose(ref_out, sol_out, atol=0.05, rtol=0.05)
            if not ok:
                diff = (ref_out.float() - sol_out.float()).abs().max().item()
                print(f'FAIL: seq_len={seq_len} scale={scale} seed={seed} max_diff={diff:.6f}')
                all_ok = False
                break
        else:
            print(f'OK: seq_len={seq_len} scale={scale}')

if all_ok:
    print('ALL TESTS PASSED')
else:
    print('SOME TESTS FAILED')
" 2>&1

The two-pass approach has one drawback: it reads V twice (or more precisely, K once and V twice, since K and V are co-located in the same cache lines). Let me consider if I can avoid the second pass. One approach: load BOTH K and V in the first pass, store scores, then use the per-page V buffer for accumulation. The V buffer is reused for each page, so I need to re-access it page by page. Wait, I think there's a better approach for the second pass. Instead of re-reading V from global memory, I can store the page V in shared memory in the first pass (while loading K), then also store it into a small ring buffer or directly accumulate after softmax. But the issue is that softmax needs ALL scores before it can compute weights. So I can't accumulate V until after all scores are known. Alternative: store K+V for ALL pages in shared memory. This requires `page_size * num_kv_heads * (head_dim*2) * num_pages * 2` bytes. For seq_len=1024: 16 * 8 * 256 * 64 * 2 = 16 * 8 * 256 * 128 = 4,194,304 bytes = 4MB. Way too much. Alternative: Use global memory as a V buffer. Store V for all tokens in global memory during the first pass, then read it back for accumulation. This trades shared memory for global memory bandwidth (but avoids a redundant read from the original kv_cache). Since the original V data is already in global memory (kv_cache), re-reading from there is the same as reading from a global memory buffer. So this doesn't help. Actually, re-reading V from the original kv_cache (second pass) does help if the first pass already brought V pages into L2 cache. The second pass would hit in L2 for those pages. So the extra memory traffic is all L2-cached (if the pages fit in L2). Another optimization: in the first pass, load BOTH K and V. Even though V isn't used for score computation, loading both brings V into L2 cache. Then in the second pass, V is in L2. Let me update the first pass to load both K and V. This adds a little extra global memory load in the first pass but ensures V is in L2 for the second pass. Actually, wait. If I load both K and V in the first pass, then the first pass reads both K and V. The second pass re-reads V. So total reads: 1xK + 2xV. Without loading V in the first pass: 1xK + 2xV = 1xK + 2xV (same). The only difference is whether the first V read is "free" (co-located with K in the same cache line) or not. Since K and V are packed together in memory ([K|V] contiguous), loading one automatically brings the other into L2 cache due to the cache line granularity. So loading K in the first pass already brings V into L2! The second V read would hit L1 or L2 cache. OK, so the two-pass approach should be reasonably efficient. The key performance question is: does the second V pass hit in L2 or L1 cache? With L1 cache size of 128KB per SM and the shared memory also taking 24KB, the total L1+shared is 128KB. If we configure L1 to be 128-24=104KB, that's enough for some pages but not all. Actually, Ampere SM has a unified 128KB L1/shared memory space. In our case, we use 24KB for shared memory, leaving 104KB for L1 data cache. Each page of K+V is 4096*2 = 8KB. 104KB / 8KB ≈ 13 pages. With 64 pages for seq_len=1024, only the most recent ~13 pages stay in L1. So the second pass may miss L1 for older pages but hit L2 (6MB on RTX 3090). So for seq_len≤1536 (4 * 16 * 8 * 256 * 2 = 1572864 bytes → 96 pages * 64KB = 6MB, right at the L2 limit), the full KV cache should fit in L2. The second pass should mostly hit L2. Good. For seq_len=4096: 256 pages * 64KB = 16MB, which exceeds L2. So the second pass would have DRAM reads. But the first pass also has DRAM reads for the same pages. The second pass adds extra DRAM traffic. For memory-bound kernel, this is suboptimal. Let me consider if I can avoid the second pass entirely by using a more numerically stable online softmax. Let me reconsider... Oh wait, I think the issue was that I changed `running_max`, `running_sum`, `acc` to double, but the SOFTMAX COMPUTATION itself (exp of scores) was still in float. The scores were computed and stored in float. The issue might be that the precision loss happens in the rescaling of the float-level softmax numerator, and the double accumulator doesn't fix this because the NUMERATORS themselves are imprecise floats. Hmm, but the softmax numerators are just exp(score - max). If score and max are both floats, and the exp operation preserves float precision, then the numerator in float should be fine for softmax. The precision loss happens when summing many float values together, which the double accumulator fixes. import torch
import reference, solution

all_ok = True

        B = 1; H = 4; Hkv = 2; D = 64; P = 16
        reference.BATCH = B; reference.NUM_HEADS = H; reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = Hkv

        sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).cuda().eval()
        sol_model.load_state_dict(ref_model.state_dict(), strict=True)
        for seed in range(3):
            torch.manual_seed(seed); torch.cuda.manual_seed_all(seed)
            inputs = [t.cuda() for t in reference.get_inputs()]
            inputs[0] = inputs[0] * scale
            inputs[1] = inputs[1] * scale
            with torch.no_grad():
                ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
                sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
            ok = torch.allclose(ref_out, sol_out, atol=0.05, rtol=0.05)
            if not ok:
                diff = (ref_out.float() - sol_out.float()).abs().max().item()
                print(f'FAIL: seq_len={seq_len} scale={scale} seed={seed} max_diff={diff:.6f}')
                all_ok = False
                break

import ctypes, torch, math
lib = ctypes.CDLL('./kernel.so')
lib.launch_paged_attention.argtypes = [ctypes.c_void_p]*5 + [ctypes.c_int]*6 + [ctypes.c_float, ctypes.c_void_p]
lib.launch_paged_attention.restype = ctypes.c_int

# Simple test: B=1, H=1, Hkv=1, D=64, L=16, P=16
B=1; H=1; Hkv=1; D=64; L=16; P=16; max_blocks=2

query = torch.zeros(B,H,D,dtype=torch.bfloat16,device='cuda')
query[0,0,0] = 1.0
num_blocks = 64
kv = torch.zeros(num_blocks,P,Hkv,2*D,dtype=torch.bfloat16,device='cuda')
kv[3,0,0,0] = 1.0; kv[3,0,0,D] = 2.0
bt = torch.tensor([[3,0]], dtype=torch.int32, device='cuda')
sl = torch.tensor([1], dtype=torch.int32, device='cuda')
out = torch.empty(B,H,D,dtype=torch.bfloat16,device='cuda')

err = lib.launch_paged_attention(
    ctypes.c_void_p(query.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(kv.data_ptr()),
    ctypes.c_void_p(bt.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(sl.data_ptr()),
    ctypes.c_void_p(out.data_ptr()),
    B, H, Hkv, D, P, max_blocks, ctypes.c_float(1.0/math.sqrt(D)), ctypes.c_void_p(0))
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'err={err} out[0,0,0]={out[0,0,0].item():.6f}')

But first, let me just check if the kernel hangs by manually running a very short CUDA program. Actually, let me take a step back. The test was running (Python had spawned several threads). This suggests it wasn't stuck on initialization. It might be that the kernel itself is in an infinite loop or waiting on something. Let me review the two-pass kernel code again for potential infinite loops: ```cuda // Pass 1 for (int pg = 0; pg < num_pages; ++pg) { ... __syncthreads(); ... __syncthreads(); } // Softmax for (int i = tid; i < seq_len; i += num_threads) { global_max = fmaxf(global_max, scores[i]); } ``` The `__syncthreads()` can deadlock if threads diverge. Looking at the code more carefully: In Pass 1, the `__syncthreads()` is inside the for loop. All threads must reach it. The threads that have `batch_idx >= batch` return early. But `batch_idx` is the same for all threads in a block (it's computed from `blockIdx.x`). So either all threads participate or none do. Good. But wait — there's a condition: `if (tid < tok_this_page)` for the cross-warp reduction. Threads with `tid >= tok_this_page` don't execute this branch. But they still reach the `__syncthreads()`. So no divergence. Good. For the softmax loop: `for (int i = tid; i < seq_len; i += num_threads)` — different threads execute different numbers of iterations, but there's no `__syncthreads()` inside this loop. After the loop, there's a warp reduction which also doesn't need sync. Good. For the warp reduction after the softmax loop: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { global_max = fmaxf(global_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, global_max, off)); } if (lane == 0) scratch[warp_id] = global_max; __syncthreads(); ``` The `__shfl_down_sync` requires that all threads in the warp are convergent (same mask). Since this code is reached by all threads and there's no branching before it, all threads in a warp are convergent. Good. Wait, actually there IS potential divergence: `for (int i = tid; i < seq_len; i += num_threads)` executes different numbers of iterations for different threads. But there's no `__syncthreads()` inside, so it's fine. After the loop, all threads converge. But what about this: `scratch[warp_id]` is written only by `lane == 0`. Multiple lanes in different warps write to different `warp_id` values. This should be fine since `warp_id = tid / 32` and each warp's lane 0 writes to `scratch[warp_id]`. All threads NOT in warp 0 don't participate in the broadcast after the `__syncthreads()`. The final `global_max = scratch[0]` is read by all threads after the cross-warp reduction. But what about threads in warps > 1? They just read `scratch[0]` which was written by thread 0 of warp 0. This should be visible after `__syncthreads()`. Good. Hmm, but the max reduction has a subtlety. Let me trace the second stage: ```cuda if (tid < num_warps) { float v = scratch[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { v = fmaxf(v, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off)); } if (tid == 0) scratch[0] = v; } __syncthreads(); global_max = scratch[0]; ``` Only `tid < num_warps` threads execute this. `num_warps = head_dim / 32 = 64 / 32 = 2`. So only threads 0 and 1 execute. These are both in warp 0. The shuffle `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off)` uses mask `0xffffffff` (all lanes). But only lanes 0 and 1 have valid `v` values. Lanes 2..31 have uninitialized `v` (or 0 depending on the compiler). The reduction with `fmaxf` would compare valid values with garbage values. If the garbage values are large positive numbers, the max would be wrong. But for max reduction, if the garbage values happen to be large, the max would be garbage. Wait, garbage values in lanes 2..31 are the issue! The variable `v` is only set for `tid < num_warps = 2`. For `tid >= 2`, `v` is... what? In the CUDA code: ```cuda if (tid < num_warps) { float v = scratch[tid]; // ... } ``` For `tid >= 2`, the code inside the `if` is NOT executed. But the `v` variable declared inside the if has limited scope. After the if block, `v` doesn't exist. So there's no problem with uninitialized values. But wait, ALL threads reach the `__syncthreads()`. Some threads (tid=2..63) don't read `scratch[0]` because the `global_max = scratch[0]` is after the sync and all threads execute it. For threads 2..63: they skip the if, do `__syncthreads()`, then read `scratch[0]`. After `__syncthreads()`, all memory writes before the sync are visible. `scratch[0]` was written by thread 0. All threads read it. This is correct. So no hang there. +++ b//home/infatoshi/kernelbench.com/benchmarks/hard/outputs/runs/20260618_153932_deepseek-claude_deepseek-v4-pro_03_paged_attention/repo/problems/03_paged_attention/test_kernel.cu

+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+#include <cstdio>
+#include <cmath>
+
+extern "C" int launch_paged_attention(
+    void* query_ptr, void* kv_cache_ptr, void* block_table_ptr,
+    void* seq_lens_ptr, void* output_ptr,
+    int batch, int num_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim,
+    int page_size, int max_blocks, float scale, cudaStream_t stream);
+
+int main() {
+    int B=1, H=1, Hkv=1, D=64, L=16, P=16, max_blocks=2;
+    float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(64.0f);
+
+    __nv_bfloat16 *query, *kv, *out;
+    int *bt, *sl;
+
+    cudaMalloc(&query, B*H*D*sizeof(__nv_bfloat16));
+    cudaMalloc(&kv, 64*P*Hkv*2*D*sizeof(__nv_bfloat16));
+    cudaMalloc(&bt, B*max_blocks*sizeof(int));
+    cudaMalloc(&sl, B*sizeof(int));
+    cudaMalloc(&out, B*H*D*sizeof(__nv_bfloat16));
+
+    cudaMemset(query, 0, B*H*D*sizeof(__nv_bfloat16));
+    cudaMemset(kv, 0, 64*P*Hkv*2*D*sizeof(__nv_bfloat16));
+    cudaMemset(out, 0, B*H*D*sizeof(__nv_bfloat16));
+
+    // query[0,0,0] = 1.0f
+    float qv = 1.0f;
+    cudaMemcpy(query, &qv, sizeof(float), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice);
+
+    // kv[3,0,0,0] = 1.0f (K), kv[3,0,0,D] = 2.0f (V)
+    float kv_k = 1.0f, kv_v = 2.0f;
+    cudaMemcpy(kv + 3*P*Hkv*2*D + 0*Hkv*2*D + 0*2*D + 0, &kv_k, sizeof(float), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice);
+    cudaMemcpy(kv + 3*P*Hkv*2*D + 0*Hkv*2*D + 0*2*D + D, &kv_v, sizeof(float), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice);
+
+    int bt_val[2] = {3, 0};
+    int sl_val = 1;
+    cudaMemcpy(bt, bt_val, 2*sizeof(int), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice);
+    cudaMemcpy(sl, &sl_val, sizeof(int), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice);
+
+    int err = launch_paged_attention(
+        query, kv, bt, sl, out,
+        B, H, Hkv, D, P, max_blocks, scale, 0);
+
+    cudaDeviceSynchronize();
+    printf("err=%d\n", err);
+
+    float result;
+    cudaMemcpy(&result, out, sizeof(float), cudaMemcpyDeviceToHost);
+    printf("out[0,0,0] = %f\n", result);
+    printf("Expected: ~2.0 (softmax of single token = 1.0, times V=2.0)\n");
+
+    cudaFree(query); cudaFree(kv); cudaFree(bt); cudaFree(sl); cudaFree(out);
+    return 0;
+}

reasoning (8197 chars)
The standalone C test is also hanging. This confirms the kernel has a bug — most likely an infinite loop. The `cudaDeviceSynchronize()` never returns, meaning the kernel is still running on the GPU. Let me kill the test and debug the kernel. The most likely cause of a hang: 1. Infinite loop (condition never becomes false) 2. Deadlock on `__syncthreads()` 3. Out-of-bounds memory access causing undefined behavior Let me look at the softmax code more carefully. After the grid-stride loop for finding the max: ```cuda // Warp+shared reduction for max #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { global_max = fmaxf(global_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, global_max, off)); } ``` Wait — I have TWO reduction stages for max (and for sum). Let me check if both are needed or if there's a conflict. First stage (warp reduction): ```cuda // Warp+shared reduction for max #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { global_max = fmaxf(global_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, global_max, off)); } if (lane == 0) scratch[warp_id] = global_max; __syncthreads(); ``` After this: `scratch[warp_id]` has the max of warp `warp_id`. Threads in the same warp have the same `scratch[warp_id]` value if `warp_id < num_warps`, otherwise scratch is uninitialized. Second stage (cross-warp): ```cuda if (tid < num_warps) { float v = scratch[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { v = fmaxf(v, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off)); } if (tid == 0) scratch[0] = v; } __syncthreads(); global_max = scratch[0]; ``` This should work for num_warps=2 (head_dim=64). Only threads 0 and 1 execute. Thread 0 reads scratch[0], thread 1 reads scratch[1]. They shuffle-reduce. Thread 0 writes scratch[0]. All threads then read scratch[0]. But there's a subtle issue: the `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off)` in the second stage. All 32 lanes in warp 0 participate. Lanes 2..31 have garbage values of `v` (they never entered the `if (tid < num_warps)` block). Wait, the variable `v` is declared INSIDE the `if` block, so it's scoped to that block. Lanes 2..31 don't have `v`. But `__shfl_down_sync` requires all lanes in the mask to participate. If some lanes don't participate, the behavior is undefined. THIS IS THE BUG! The mask `0xffffffff` includes all 32 lanes, but only lanes 0 and 1 have valid `v`. Lanes 2..31 have garbage/uninitialized values. The shuffle with undefined values is undefined behavior in CUDA. The fix: either use a sub-mask for the shuffle, or ensure all lanes have valid (dummy) values. For the max reduction, I can initialize all lanes' v values: ```cuda float v = -1e30f; // dummy for lanes >= num_warps if (tid < num_warps) { v = scratch[tid]; } ``` Or use a mask: ```cuda unsigned mask = (1u << num_warps) - 1; float v = scratch[tid]; // tid < num_warps means valid for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { v = fmaxf(v, __shfl_down_sync(mask, v, off)); } ``` Wait, the mask approach is tricky because `__shfl_down_sync` requires ALL lanes in the mask to participate. If num_warps=2, mask=0x3 means only lanes 0 and 1. Lane 0 shuffles down to get lane 1's value (off=1). Then lane 0 has the max. But lane 0 shuffling down with off=2 would try to get lane 2's value, but lane 2 is not in the mask. This is also undefined. The safest fix: initialize all lanes with dummy values before the if block. But actually, I think the REAL hang might be elsewhere. Let me check if the warp-split causes a deadlock via __syncthreads(). Looking more carefully at the second reduction stage: ```cuda if (tid < num_warps) { float v = scratch[tid]; // ... shuffle ... if (tid == 0) scratch[0] = v; } __syncthreads(); ``` Threads 0 and 1 enter the if block, execute shuffle, thread 0 writes scratch[0]. Threads 2..63 skip the if block entirely. All threads then hit `__syncthreads()`. This is fine — no divergence across the sync barrier (the if-else is complete before the sync). But the shuffle inside the if block: only threads 0 and 1 execute it. The shuffle `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off)` expects ALL 32 lanes in warp 0 to participate. But only lanes 0 and 1 are executing the shuffle. Lanes 2..31 in warp 0 are NOT executing the shuffle — they're past the if block (waiting at __syncthreads?). Wait no, that's wrong. `__syncthreads()` is a barrier — ALL threads in the block must reach it before any proceed. But the code inside the `if (tid < num_warps)` block does NOT contain a `__syncthreads()`. The `__syncthreads()` is AFTER the if block. So: - Threads 0 and 1: enter if, do shuffle, write scratch, exit if, hit __syncthreads() - Threads 2..31: skip if, hit __syncthreads() Wait, threads 2..31 in warp 0 skip the ENTIRE if block (since `tid >= num_warps = 2`). They go directly to `__syncthreads()`. But the shuffle `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off)` inside the if block requires ALL lanes in the mask (0xffffffff = 0..31) to participate. Lanes 2..31 are NOT executing the shuffle — they're waiting at `__syncthreads()`. According to the CUDA programming guide: `__shfl_sync` requires all threads in the mask to execute the same instruction. If some threads in the mask don't execute it, the behavior is UNDEFINED. This is undefined behavior, and it can cause hangs. THIS IS THE BUG! The shuffle mask includes lanes that are not executing the shuffle, which causes undefined behavior (likely a hang). Let me fix this by ensuring all lanes in the warp participate in the shuffle. The simplest fix: move the initialization of `v` outside the if block: ```cuda float v = (tid < num_warps) ? scratch[tid] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { v += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, v, off); } if (tid == 0) scratch[0] = v; __syncthreads(); ``` This way ALL 32 lanes participate in the shuffle. Lanes 2..31 have v=0.0f, which doesn't affect the sum (for sum reduction) or the max (for max reduction with v=-1e30f). But wait, `__shfl_down_sync` requires that the MASK argument matches exactly across all threads calling it. With `0xffffffff`, all 32 lanes must call it. If I move the initialization outside the if, ALL threads call the shuffle, and the mask matches. This fixes the issue. Similarly for the first reduction stage: ```cuda // Warp+shared reduction for max #pragma unroll for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { global_max = fmaxf(global_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, global_max, off)); } ``` Here, ALL threads execute this code (it's not inside any if block). All threads have `global_max` (initialized from the grid-stride loop). For threads where `tid >= seq_len` and `global_max` wasn't updated in the grid-stride loop, `global_max = -1e30f`. This is a valid dummy value for max reduction. All 32 lanes participate. This is OK. So the fix is in the SECOND reduction stage only. Let me fix both the max and sum reductions. Actually, looking at the first reduction stage more carefully: ```cuda if (lane == 0) scratch[warp_id] = global_max; ``` ALL threads execute the shuffle. Only lane 0 writes. This is fine because `__shfl_down_sync` is before the write, and all threads participate. ✓ For the second stage: ```cuda float v = (tid < num_warps) ? scratch[tid] : -1e30f; // max // or float v = (tid < num_warps) ? scratch[tid] : 0.0f; // sum ``` Actually using a ternary like `v = (tid < num_warps) ? scratch[tid] : -1e30f;` might still cause divergent behavior for `__shfl_down_sync` because the mask argument must be the same across all threads in the warp. But the ternary is evaluated BEFORE the shuffle, so all threads call shuffle with the same mask `0xffffffff`. The values differ (some are valid, some are dummy), but the shuffle instruction is executed by all threads. This should work. Wait, but there's another issue: `scratch[tid]` for `tid >= num_warps` reads shared memory that might contain garbage (not written to previously). This could be a problem with the read itself (though shared memory reads don't hang). So as long as the shuffle is called by all threads, it should work. Let me fix the kernel code now. I need to fix BOTH the max reduction and the sum reduction second stages.

With GQA (Hkv=8), all 8 kv_heads are processed independently. Actually, the kernel processes ONE kv_head at a time (the one needed for the current query head). So each query head reads only 1/8 of the KV data. For B=8, H=32: 256 blocks, each reading 1024 * 128 * 2 * 3 = 768KB (K once + V twice). Total per block: 768KB. Total across all blocks: 256 * 768KB = 192MB. But there's GQA — each KV head is read by G=4 query heads. So the same KV data is read 4 times (once for each head in the group). This wastes bandwidth. Total KV cache data: 8 batches * 1024 tokens * 8 kv_heads * 128 dim * 2 (K+V) * 2 bytes = 8 * 1024 * 8 * 128 * 2 * 2 = 33.6MB. This means the same data is read ~5.7 times (due to GQA sharing and the extra V pass). This is very inefficient! A better approach would be to fuse all query heads in a GQA group into one threadblock, so the KV data is read once per group. Same error again! The online softmax with double precision still fails. The error is IDENTICAL to before (0.0935059, bad=14/32768). This is very suspicious — the error is the SAME regardless of whether I use float or double for the running state. This means the issue is NOT in the online softmax at all. It's somewhere else in the computation. Let me think about what could cause an identical error regardless of precision changes. Wait — what if the error is in the DOT PRODUCT computation? The warp shuffle reduction for the dot product might be incorrect when num_warps < 4 or when head_dim doesn't evenly divide by WARP_SIZE. Actually, looking more carefully, for head_dim=128: num_warps=4, num_threads=128. The warp reduction stores `scratch[pos * num_warps + warp_id]` for each position and warp. Then the cross-warp reduction sums them: `score = scratch[tid * num_warps + w]` for `w < num_warps`. This is correct. But wait — when `group_size > 1` (fused GQA), the inner loop iterates over query heads. For `g=0`, it uses `scratch[]` for reductions. For `g=1`, it also uses `scratch[]` — REUSING the same memory. The `__syncthreads()` at the end of the outer loop iteration ensures all writes from head g are visible before head g+1 starts. But does this work correctly? Let me trace: - Head g=0: writes to scratch[tid] (scores), scratch[page_size + warp_id] (warp sums), scratch[page_size + num_warps] (page sum). All these are in the scratch area. - `__syncthreads()` after head g=0. All writes visible. - Head g=1: reuses ALL the same scratch locations. Head g=0's data is overwritten. - Accumulation reads scratch[pos] which was just written by head g=1. This should be fine — each head independently computes its own scores, softmax, and accumulation. But there's a potential issue: the `scratch[tid] = score` write (during cross-warp reduction) is only executed by `tid < tok_this_page`. For `tid >= tok_this_page`, `scratch[tid]` retains the PREVIOUS head's value! Then when head g=1 reads `scratch[pos]` for accumulation, it might read head g=0's residual value for positions where `pos >= tok_this_page`. Wait, that can't be the issue because the accumulation loop only reads for `pos < tok_this_page`: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int pos = 0; pos < tok_this_page; ++pos) { my_acc += (double)scratch[pos] * (double)__bfloat162float(v_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]); } ``` So only positions 0..tok_this_page-1 are read, and those were written by head g=1. Head g=0's residual values at indices >= tok_this_page are never read. But what about the SCORE computation? In head g=1: ```cuda if (tid < tok_this_page) { float score = 0.0f; for (int w = 0; w < num_warps; ++w) { score += scratch[tid * num_warps + w]; } score *= scale; scratch[tid] = score; page_max = score; } ``` This reads `scratch[tid * num_warps + w]` which was written by the warp reduction for head g=1 (not head g=0, because there was a `__syncthreads()` between them and the warp reduction happened before this). So the data should be correct. Hmm, let me think about this more. Maybe the issue is that the `partial[pos]` values for head g=1 are computed from `k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]` using `q_vals[1]`. The K data in shared memory hasn't changed (it's the same for all heads in the group). So the dot products should be correct. Wait, actually I just realized something. I can process one head at a time (all scores for one head, then softmax, then accumulate) and reuse the scores buffer. The actual data moved is: - Shape 0 (B=8, H=32, D=128, L=1024, Hkv=8): - Bytes formula: 2*B*L*Hkv*D*2 + B*H*D*2*2 = 2*8*1024*8*128*2 + 8*32*128*2*2 - KV: 2*8*1024*8*128*2 = 33,554,432 bytes = 32 MB - Query+output: 8*32*128*4 = 131,072 bytes = 128 KB - Total: ~32 MB With my kernel (two-pass): KV read once for K + twice for V = 3 KV reads = 3 * 16 MB per direction = 48 MB streamed. The benchmark's bytes_moved formula is slightly different: ``` bytes_formula: 2 * batch * seq_len * num_kv_heads * head_dim * 2 + batch * num_heads * head_dim * 2 * 2 ``` = 2 * 8 * 1024 * 8 * 128 * 2 + 8 * 32 * 128 * 4 = 33,554,432 + 131,072 = 33,685,504 bytes At 0.892 ms: 33.7 MB / 0.892 ms = 37.8 GB/s. peak_fraction = 37.8/936 = 0.040. Still not great. For the single-pass online softmax approach: only 2 KV reads (K+V once). Data moved = 2 * 16 MB = 32 MB. At same speed: 32 MB / 0.892ms = 35.8 GB/s. But we'd get 0.040 * 1.5 = 0.060 peak fraction (since the formula counts 2x KV reads). Hmm, still below 0.1. I think the real issue is that my kernel isn't effectively saturating memory bandwidth. The 3090 has 936 GB/s theoretical peak, but effective bandwidth for random-ish reads is typically 70-80% = 650-750 GB/s. My kernel achieves ~113 GB/s which is only 15% of that. Let me think about why. The main bottlenecks: 1. **Small threadblocks**: 64-128 threads, but each thread does only 2-byte loads. With 128 threads doing 2-byte loads, each load wave moves only 256 bytes. 2. **Strided access**: K and V for a single kv_head are strided by num_kv_heads * head_dim * 2 elements. This creates non-coalesced access patterns across positions. 3. **Synchronization overhead**: Many __syncthreads() calls per page. To improve memory throughput, I should use vectorized loads (float4 = 8 bf16 values per load). With float4 loads, each thread loads 16 bytes at once. With 32 threads loading float4, we get 512 bytes per load wave — much better for coalescing. But the strided access pattern is harder to optimize. For a given position p, the K data is contiguous within head_dim, so a float4 load works. But between positions, the stride is num_kv_heads * head_dim * 2 elements. Let me try a different memory access pattern: instead of having one thread handle one head_dim element across all positions, have each thread handle a set of CONTIGUOUS elements for each position. For example, with 32 threads and head_dim=128: each thread loads 4 consecutive elements (a float4) per position. This gives good coalescing within a position. The 16 positions require 16 iterations, each loading 32 float4s = 512 bytes. This is 2 L1 cache lines per position, which is efficient. Actually, the current design already does this: each thread loads `k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]` where `tid` is consecutive (0..127 for D=128). Within a given position `pos`, consecutive threads load consecutive memory addresses. This IS coalesced! The issue is that between positions, the same thread loads from a different position with a large stride. This causes L1 cache misses. OK so the fundamental issue is the strided access between positions. This is inherent to the KV cache layout and can't be avoided without changing the layout. The vLLM/FlashInfer kernels also deal with this. Given that we're dealing with a fixed layout and need to get above 0.1, let me try to use more aggressive memory access: load the FULL PAGE (all kv_heads and both K+V) into shared memory. With page_size=16, num_kv_heads=8, 2*D=256: 16 * 8 * 256 * 2 = 65536 bytes = 64KB per page. But this exceeds shared memory (48KB). Even with 100KB, only 1 page fits. This doesn't help with the striding issue. Alternative: process all kv_heads in a SINGLE threadblock. The block processes all H query heads for a batch element, not just one head. This way, all kv_heads' K data is needed, and loading the full page (or all kv_heads) makes sense. Actually, an elegant approach: have one block per BATCH element (not per head). The block processes ALL H heads for one batch element. It loads the FULL page (all kv_heads, K+V) into shared memory. Then all query heads can use this data. But the full page is 64KB, which needs the opt-in 100KB shared memory. With 100KB per block and 82 SMs, we can run at most 82 blocks concurrently. For B=32, we have 32 blocks — all can run concurrently. This approach: - 1 block per batch element (B blocks total) - Loads full page (64KB) into shared memory, processes all H=32 heads - KV loaded ONCE, shared across all heads (32x less KV reads compared to per-head!) Wait, but processing 32 heads requires 32 * 128 = 4096 threads. That's 32 warps, too many for a threadblock. Each threadblock has max 1024 threads. I could use warp-level processing: assign each warp to one head. With 32 warps and 32 heads, that's one warp per head. But each warp needs to handle head_dim=128 elements with only 32 threads (4 elements per thread). This is doable. But 32 warps * 32 threads = 1024 threads, which is the max. And each warp processes one head. This is getting complex. Let me try a simpler approach: just improve memory throughput by using vectorized loads in the existing per-head kernel. Instead of 1 bf16 per thread per position, use float4 (8 bf16 values). This reduces the number of threads needed to 16 (for D=128) or 8 (for D=64). Actually, let me try running the benchmark with the current kernel and see what the score is. It passed correctness before at 0.025. Maybe I should accept that and focus on other improvements later. But the requirement is "push the number up" and 0.025 is LOW. Wait, I need to actually run the benchmark. Let me compile and run.

+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+
+#define WARP_SIZE 32
+
+// Single-pass online softmax with fp64 running state for numerical stability.
+// One threadblock per (batch, head). Each thread handles one head_dim element.
+
+extern "C" __global__ void paged_attention_kernel(
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query,
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache,
+    const int* __restrict__ block_table,
+    const int* __restrict__ seq_lens,
+    __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output,
+    const int batch, const int num_heads, const int num_kv_heads,
+    const int head_dim, const int page_size,
+    const int max_blocks, const float scale)
+{
+    const int bid = blockIdx.x;
+    const int batch_idx = bid / num_heads;
+    const int head_idx  = bid % num_heads;
+    if (batch_idx >= batch) return;
+
+    const int group_size = num_heads / num_kv_heads;
+    const int kv_head    = head_idx / group_size;
+    const int seq_len    = seq_lens[batch_idx];
+    const int num_pages  = (seq_len + page_size - 1) / page_size;
+
+    const int tid = threadIdx.x, num_threads = blockDim.x;
+    const int num_warps = num_threads / WARP_SIZE;
+    const int lane = tid % WARP_SIZE, warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE;
+
+    extern __shared__ __align__(16) char smem_buf[];
+    __nv_bfloat16* k_smem = (__nv_bfloat16*)smem_buf;
+    __nv_bfloat16* v_smem = k_smem + page_size * head_dim;
+    float* scratch = (float*)(v_smem + page_size * head_dim);
+
+    const int stride_blk = page_size * num_kv_heads * (head_dim * 2);
+    const int stride_pos = num_kv_heads * (head_dim * 2);
+    const int stride_kvh = head_dim * 2;
+
+    const float q_val = __bfloat162float(
+        query[batch_idx * num_heads * head_dim + head_idx * head_dim + tid]);
+
+    // fp64 running state for online softmax
+    double running_max = -1e30;
+    double running_sum = 0.0;
+    double acc = 0.0;
+
+    const int* bt = block_table + batch_idx * max_blocks;
+
+    for (int pg = 0; pg < num_pages; ++pg) {
+        const int blk_id = bt[pg];
+        const int tok_this_page =
+            (pg == num_pages - 1) ? (seq_len - pg * page_size) : page_size;
+
+        // Cooperative load K+V
+        const int base = blk_id * stride_blk + kv_head * stride_kvh;
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < page_size; ++pos) {
+            const int off = base + pos * stride_pos;
+            if (pos < tok_this_page) {
+                k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = kv_cache[off + tid];
+                v_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = kv_cache[off + head_dim + tid];
+            } else {
+                k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = __float2bfloat16(0.0f);
+                v_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = __float2bfloat16(0.0f);
+            }
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // Dot products
+        float partial[16];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < page_size; ++pos)
+            partial[pos] = q_val * __bfloat162float(k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]);
+
+        // Warp reduce
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < page_size; ++pos) {
+            float val = partial[pos];
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1)
+                val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, off);
+            if (lane == 0) scratch[pos * num_warps + warp_id] = val;
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // Cross-warp → scores, find page max (float)
+        float page_max_f = -1e30f;
+        if (tid < tok_this_page) {
+            float score = 0.0f;
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int w = 0; w < num_warps; ++w)
+                score += scratch[tid * num_warps + w];
+            score *= scale;
+            scratch[tid] = score;
+            page_max_f = score;
+        }
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1)
+            page_max_f = fmaxf(page_max_f, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, page_max_f, off));
+        page_max_f = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, page_max_f, 0);
+
+        // Online softmax update (fp64)
+        double new_max = fmax(running_max, (double)page_max_f);
+        double rescale = exp(running_max - new_max);
+        running_sum = running_sum * rescale;
+        acc = acc * rescale;
+
+        // Compute softmax numerators (float)
+        if (tid < tok_this_page) {
+            float prob = expf(scratch[tid] - (float)new_max);
+            scratch[tid] = prob;
+        }
+
+        // Warp-reduce sum of probs
+        float my_sum = 0.0f;
+        if (tid < tok_this_page) my_sum = scratch[tid];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1)
+            my_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, my_sum, off);
+        if (lane == 0) scratch[page_size + warp_id] = my_sum;
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // Cross-warp sum → fp64 running_sum update
+        float page_sum = (tid < num_warps) ? scratch[page_size + tid] : 0.0f;
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1)
+            page_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, page_sum, off);
+        if (tid == 0) scratch[page_size + num_warps] = page_sum;
+        __syncthreads();
+        running_sum += (double)scratch[page_size + num_warps];
+
+        // Accumulate (fp64)
+        double my_acc = 0.0;
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < tok_this_page; ++pos)
+            my_acc += (double)scratch[pos] *
+                      (double)__bfloat162float(v_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]);
+        acc += my_acc;
+
+        running_max = new_max;
+        __syncthreads();
+    }
+
+    float result = (running_sum > 1e-30) ? (float)(acc / running_sum) : 0.0f;
+    output[batch_idx * num_heads * head_dim + head_idx * head_dim + tid] =
+        __float2bfloat16(result);
+}
+
+
+exte

import sys; sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch, math, numpy as np
import reference

reference.BATCH=8; reference.NUM_HEADS=32; reference.NUM_KV_HEADS=8
reference.HEAD_DIM=128; reference.SEQ_LEN=1024; reference.PAGE_SIZE=16

torch.manual_seed(123); torch.cuda.manual_seed_all(123)

b=2; h=27; D=128; Hkv=8; kvh=6; L=1024; P=16; scale=1.0/math.sqrt(D)
q = inputs[0][b,h].float() * 8.0  # large_q scaled
kv_cache = inputs[1] * 8.0
bt = inputs[2][b]
num_pages = (L+P-1)//P
pages = bt[:num_pages].long()
kv = kv_cache.index_select(0,pages).reshape(num_pages*P, Hkv, 2*D)[:L]
k_ref = kv[:,kvh,:D].float()
v_ref = kv[:,kvh,D:].float()

# Reference scores
ref_scores = (q @ k_ref.T) * scale

# Simulate warp-shuffle dot product CUDA reduction
q_np = q.numpy().astype(np.float32)
k_np = k_ref.numpy().astype(np.float32)
N_WARPS = D // 32  # 4
my_scores = np.zeros(L, np.float32)
for tok in range(L):
    partial = q_np * k_np[tok]
    warp_sums = np.zeros(N_WARPS, np.float32)
    for w in range(N_WARPS):
        wp = partial[w*32:(w+1)*32].copy()
        for off in [16,8,4,2,1]:
            for i in range(32-off):
                wp[i] += wp[i+off]
        warp_sums[w] = wp[0]
    my_scores[tok] = np.sum(warp_sums) * scale

score_diff = np.abs(ref_scores.numpy().astype(np.float32) - my_scores)
print(f'Score diff: max={score_diff.max():.12f} mean={score_diff.mean():.12f}')

# Compare top-k
k=5
ref_top = np.argsort(ref_scores.numpy())[-k:][::-1]
my_top = np.argsort(my_scores)[-k:][::-1]
print(f'Top-{k}: ref={ref_top} my={my_top} match={np.array_equal(ref_top, my_top)}')

# Now check softmax both ways
ref_soft = torch.softmax(ref_scores, dim=-1).numpy()
my_max = my_scores.max()
my_exp = np.exp(my_scores - my_max).astype(np.float64)
my_soft = (my_exp / my_exp.sum()).astype(np.float32)
soft_diff = np.abs(ref_soft - my_soft)
print(f'Softmax diff: max={soft_diff.max():.10f}')

# V-weighted output
ref_out = (torch.from_numpy(ref_soft) @ v_ref).numpy()
my_out = (my_soft @ v_ref.numpy()).numpy()
out_diff = np.abs(ref_out - my_out)
print(f'Output diff: max={out_diff.max():.10f}')

Let me check something else: maybe the issue is with the exp computation in the online softmax creating subnormal numbers that behave differently in CUDA vs NumPy. Wait — in the NumPy simulation, I used BATCH softmax (all scores at once). In the CUDA kernel, I use ONLINE softmax (page by page). The online softmax with fp64 should be mathematically equivalent, but maybe the implementation has a subtle bug. Let me check: in the online softmax, after computing `exp(score - new_max)` for each token in the current page, I accumulate: `acc += prob * V[pos][tid]`. But the V values are loaded from shared memory. Let me verify the shared memory V loading is correct. In the kernel, for V: ```cuda v_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = kv_cache[off + head_dim + tid]; ``` `off = base + pos * stride_pos = blk_id * stride_blk + kv_head * stride_kvh + pos * stride_pos` `kv_cache[off + head_dim + tid]` = `kv_cache[blk_id * P * Hkv * 2D + kv_head * 2D + pos * Hkv * 2D + D + tid]` This is the V element at `kv_cache[blk_id, pos, kv_head, D+tid]` which is correct. Let me read the K value: `kv_cache[off + tid]` = `kv_cache[blk_id * P * Hkv * 2D + kv_head * 2D + pos * Hkv * 2D + tid]` This is the K element at `kv_cache[blk_id, pos, kv_head, tid]` which is correct. Both K and V loads are correct. The shared memory layout is: - K: `k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]` — correct row-major order - V: `v_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]` — correct row-major order The dot product uses `k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]` and `q_val` (which is `query[..., head_dim + tid]`). This gives `sum_tid q[tid] * K[pos][tid]` which is the correct dot product. Wait... actually there IS a subtle issue here. The q_val is loaded from query as: ```cuda query[batch_idx * num_heads * head_dim + head_idx * head_dim + tid] ``` And the K is loaded from kv_cache with kv_head: ```cuda kv_cache[off + tid] // where off includes kv_head_idx ``` But in the REFERENCE: ```python k = kv[..., :D] # (L, Hkv, D) k = k.repeat_interleave(G, dim=1) # (L, H, D) ``` After repeat_interleave, `k[:, h]` for head `h` is the same as `k[:, h // G]` before repeat. So head 27 uses kv_head 6 (since 27 // 7 = 3? No, 27 // 4 = 6). In my kernel: `kv_head = head_idx / group_size`. For head_idx=27, group_size=4: `kv_head = 27 / 4 = 6`. This is integer division (C `int` division truncates toward zero). So kv_head=6. Correct. The query `q = query[b]` has shape (H, D). `q[27]` is the query for head 27. In my kernel: ```cuda query[batch_idx * num_heads * head_dim + head_idx * head_dim + tid] ``` = `query[2 * 32 * 128 + 27 * 128 + tid]`. This is correct. The reference computes: ```python q = query[b] # (H, D) qf = q.float() # (H, D) ``` Then `qf[27]` is the fp32 query for head 27. Same as my kernel. ✓ I'm completely stumped. The NumPy simulation shows the dot products and softmax are correct to 1e-7 or better. But the CUDA kernel produces errors up to 0.094. Let me try one more thing: compare the SCORES directly by modifying the kernel to output scores instead of attention output. Then compare with reference scores. Actually, a quicker approach: what if the issue is that `expf` in CUDA produces slightly different results than `exp` in NumPy? For values near zero, this shouldn't matter. But for large negative values, the difference could be amplified. Using the `--use_fast_math` flag might make `expf` use a less accurate intrinsic. But I removed `--use_fast_math`. Let me check what compiler flags I'm using... Looking at my nvcc command: `/opt/cuda/bin/nvcc -O3 -arch=sm_86 -shared -Xcompiler -fPIC -std=c++17 -o kernel_v2.so kernel_v2.cu` No `--use_fast_math`. So `expf` should use the precise math library. But there might be a difference between `expf` in CUDA vs `exp` in NumPy (which uses the CPU's libm). For the same fp32 input, `expf` on CUDA and `expf` on CPU should produce bit-identical results (IEEE 754). Unless there's a subnormal flushing issue. With the scale=8.0 case, the scores can be large negative values. For example, if the max score is 100 and another score is -10, exp(-10 - 100) = exp(-110) ≈ 2e-48. This is a subnormal number in fp32. Actually, fp32 minimum normal is ~1.18e-38. Subnormals go down to ~1.4e-45. 2e-48 is below subnormal and underflows to 0. This is fine for softmax since such small values don't affect the sum. But there's a subtle issue: CUDA's `expf` might flush subnormals to zero at a different threshold than the CPU's `expf`. This could cause slight differences in the softmax weights, but they should be tiny. OK, I'm going to try a completely different approach. Instead of debugging this further, let me just use a kernel-internal double precision dot product and softmax. This should GUARANTEE bit-identical results to the reference (which uses fp32). Actually, wait. I just had an idea. What if the issue is that my NumPy simulation of the warp shuffle is wrong? Let me check my simulation: ```python for w in range(N_WARPS): wp = partial[w*32:(w+1)*32].copy() for off in [16,8,4,2,1]: for i in range(32-off): wp[i] += wp[i+off] warp_sums[w] = wp[0] ``` In the CUDA kernel, `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, off)` does: - For lane `i` where `i + off < WARP_SIZE`: returns value from lane `i + off` - For lane `i` where `i + off >= WARP_SIZE`: returns own value And then `val += returned_value`. So lane `i` adds lane `i+off`'s value. In my simulation: `wp[i] += wp[i+off]` for `i < 32-off`. This adds element `i+off` to element `i`. But in the CUDA kernel, ALL 32 lanes execute the shuffle, not just `i < 32-off`. For `i >= 32-off`, lane `i` gets its own value (self-add = double). In the next iteration with smaller offset, this doubled value gets added to an earlier lane. Wait, that's NOT right! Let me re-read the shuffle semantics. `__shfl_down_sync(mask, var, delta)`: - Lane `i` returns `var` from lane `i + delta` if `i + delta < width` - Lane `i` returns its own `var` if `i + delta >= width` So `val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, delta)`: - Lane i where i+delta < 32: val += val_from_lane[i+delta] - Lane i where i+delta >= 32: val += val (doubles its own val!) This is different from my simulation where for `i >= 32-delta`, I do NOTHING (the element stays the same). In the CUDA kernel, those elements DOUBLE. This means my NumPy simulation is WRONG! The correct simulation should be: ```python for off in [16,8,4,2,1]: for i in range(32): src = i + off if src < 32: wp[i] += wp[src] else: wp[i] += wp[i] # self-add = double! ``` Wait, that doesn't sound right either. Let me check again. Actually, looking at the CUDA docs more carefully: `float __shfl_down_sync(unsigned mask, float var, unsigned delta, int width=warpSize)` Description: "Calculates the source lane index by subtracting delta (rather than XORing with delta as __shfl_xor_sync does). Returns the value of var held by the source lane." For `delta=16`: - Lane 0: source = 0+16 = 16 → returns var from lane 16 - Lane 1: source = 1+16 = 17 → returns var from lane 17 - ... - Lane 15: source = 15+16 = 31 → returns var from lane 31 - Lane 16: source = 16+16 = 32 ≥ 32 → returns its own var - ... - Lane 31: source = 31+16 = 47 ≥ 32 → returns its own var So for lane 16, `val += __shfl_down_sync(...)` adds lane 16's value to itself: `val = val + val = 2 * val`. This DOUBLES lane 16's value! Similarly, lane 17 doubles, ..., lane 31 doubles. Then in the next iteration (delta=8): - Lane 8: source = 8+8 = 16 → gets lane 16's value (which was DOUBLED) - Lane 9: source = 9+8 = 17 → gets lane 17's value (DOUBLED) - ... - Lane 15: source = 15+8 = 23 → gets lane 23's value (DOUBLED) - Lanes 16-23: source ≥ 24 (32?) → actually 16+8=24 < 32, so source=24. Wait, lanes 16..23 get lanes 24..31 (which were also doubled in the previous step). This self-doubling behavior is intended for parallel reductions: after the full sequence of shuffles, lane 0 has the sum of ALL 32 lanes' ORIGINAL values. Let me verify: - Original: a[0], a[1], ..., a[31] - delta=16: - lane 0: a[0] + a[16] - lane 1: a[1] + a[17] - ... - lane 15: a[15] + a[31] - lane 16: a[16] + a[16] = 2*a[16] - lane 17: a[17] + a[17] = 2*a[17] - ... - lane 31: a[31] + a[31] = 2*a[31] - delta=8: - lane 0: (a[0]+a[16]) + (a[8]+a[24]) ← gets lane 8's (a[8]+a[24]) - lane 1: (a[1]+a[17]) + (a[9]+a[25]) - ... - lane 8: (a[8]+a[24]) + (2*a[16]) ← gets lane 16's (2*a[16]) Wait, that's wrong! The original reduction is supposed to sum all elements, not introduce doublings. Hmm, this doesn't make sense. Let me re-read the shuffle semantics. Actually, looking at this more carefully, I think I misread the doc. The `__shfl_down_sync` is: - For lane i, if i + delta < warpSize, return var from lane i + delta - If i + delta >= warpSize, return var from lane i (its own value) So `val += __shfl_down_sync(val, delta)`: - if i + delta < warpSize: val += val[i+delta] - else: val += val[i] (self, which doubles it) This IS what I described. The self-doubling for trailing lanes is intentional in the reduction pattern: the doubled values cascade down to lane 0 through subsequent iterations. Let me trace a small example with 8 lanes: Original: a[0..7] delta=4: Lane 0: a[0] + a[4] Lane 1: a[1] + a[5] Lane 2: a[2] + a[6] Lane 3: a[3] + a[7] Lane 4: 2*a[4] Lane 5: 2*a[5] Lane 6: 2*a[6] Lane 7: 2*a[7] delta=2: Lane 0: (a[0]+a[4]) + (a[2]+a[6]) ← from lane 2 Lane 1: (a[1]+a[5]) + (a[3]+a[7]) Lane 2: (a[2]+a[6]) + 2*a[4] ← from lane 4 (doubled) Lane 3: (a[3]+a[7]) + 2*a[5] Lane 4: 2*a[4] + 2*a[6] ← from lane 6 Lane 5: 2*a[5] + 2*a[7] Lane 6: 2*a[6] + 2*a[6] = 4*a[6] ← self Lane 7: 2*a[7] + 2*a[7] = 4*a[7] delta=1: Lane 0: (a[0]+a[4]+a[2]+a[6]) + (a[1]+a[5]+a[3]+a[7]) = sum of all a[0..7] ✓ It works! The self-doubling for trailing lanes creates weighted values that eventually contribute correctly to lane 0. So my NumPy simulation needs to account for this self-doubling: ```python for off in [16,8,4,2,1]: for i in range(32): if i + off < 32: wp[i] += wp[i + off] else: wp[i] += wp[i] # self-add ``` But wait, in my original NumPy simulation, I did: ```python for off in [16,8,4,2,1]: for i in range(32 - off): wp[i] += wp[i+off] ``` This only adds for i < 32-off. For i >= 32-off, nothing happens (wp[i] stays the same). This is DIFFERENT from the CUDA behavior where wp[i] doubles! So the question is: does the CORRECT dot product depend on the self-doubling behavior? Let me check: after all reductions, only lane 0's value matters. The self-doubling only affects the intermediate values in higher lanes. These higher lane values get shuffled down to lower lanes in subsequent iterations, contributing correctly. In my simulation (without self-doubling): after delta=16, lanes 16-31 keep their original values. After delta=8, lanes 0-7 add lanes 8-15, and lanes 16-23 add lanes 24-31 (via shuffle from 16+8=24). This is correct. Lanes 24-31 keep their values (which is wrong — they should double). After delta=4: lane 0 adds lane 4... etc. Does the end result (lane 0) come out the same? import numpy as np, torch, math, sys; sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import reference

reference.BATCH=8; reference.NUM_HEADS=32; reference.NUM_KV_HEADS=8
reference.HEAD_DIM=128; reference.SEQ_LEN=1024; reference.PAGE_SIZE=16
torch.manual_seed(123);

inputs = [t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
b=2; h=27; D=128; Hkv=8; kvh=6; L=1024; P=16
q = (inputs[0][b,h].float()*8.0).numpy().astype(np.float32)
kv_cache = inputs[1]*8.0
bt = inputs[2][b]
num_pages = (L+P-1)//P
pages = bt[:num_pages].long()
kv = kv_cache.index_select(0,pages).reshape(num_pages*P, Hkv, 2*D)[:L]
k_ref = kv[:,kvh,:D].float().numpy().astype(np.float32)
scale = 1.0/math.sqrt(D)

# CORRECT warp shuffle simulation (with self-doubling)
N_WARPS = D // 32
scores_correct = np.zeros(L, np.float32)
for tok in range(L):
    partial = q * k_ref[tok]
    warp_sums = np.zeros(N_WARPS, np.float32)
    for w in range(N_WARPS):
        wp = partial[w*32:(w+1)*32].copy()
        for off in [16,8,4,2,1]:
            for i in range(32):
                src = i + off
                if src < 32:
                    wp[i] += wp[src]
                else:
                    wp[i] += wp[i]  # self-doubling for i+off >= 32
        warp_sums[w] = wp[0]
    scores_correct[tok] = np.sum(warp_sums) * scale

ref_scores = (torch.from_numpy(q) @ torch.from_numpy(k_ref).T * scale).numpy()
diff = np.abs(ref_scores.astype(np.float32) - scores_correct)
print(f'Score diff (correct warp sim): max={diff.max():.12f} mean={diff.mean():.12f}')

# Also check: does the OLD (incorrect) simulation give different results?
scores_wrong = np.zeros(L, np.float32)
for tok in range(L):
    partial = q * k_ref[tok]
    warp_sums = np.zeros(N_WARPS, np.float32)
    for w in range(N_WARPS):
        wp = partial[w*32:(w+1)*32].copy()
        for off in [16,8,4,2,1]:
            for i in range(32-off):
                wp[i] += wp[i+off]
        warp_sums[w] = wp[0]
    scores_wrong[tok] = np.sum(warp_sums) * scale

print(f'Score diff (wrong warp sim): max={diff_wrong.max():.12f} mean={diff_wrong.mean():.12f}')

diff_cw = np.abs(scores_correct - scores_wrong)
print(f'Correct vs wrong diff: max={diff_cw.max():.12f}')

+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+
+#define WARP_SIZE 32
+
+// Debug kernel: outputs the SCORES (pre-softmax dot products) for
+// a single (batch, head) to compare with reference.
+extern "C" __global__ void debug_scores_kernel(
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query,
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache,
+    const int* __restrict__ block_table,
+    const int* __restrict__ seq_lens,
+    float* __restrict__ scores_out,
+    const int batch_idx, const int head_idx,
+    const int num_heads, const int num_kv_heads,
+    const int head_dim, const int page_size,
+    const int max_blocks, const float scale)
+{
+    int kv_head = head_idx / (num_heads / num_kv_heads);
+    int seq_len = seq_lens[batch_idx];
+    int num_pages = (seq_len + page_size - 1) / page_size;
+
+    int tid = threadIdx.x, num_threads = blockDim.x;
+    int num_warps = num_threads / WARP_SIZE;
+    int lane = tid % WARP_SIZE, warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE;
+
+    extern __shared__ __align__(16) char smem_buf[];
+    __nv_bfloat16* k_smem = (__nv_bfloat16*)smem_buf;
+    float* scratch = (float*)(k_smem + page_size * head_dim);
+
+    int stride_blk = page_size * num_kv_heads * (head_dim * 2);
+    int stride_pos = num_kv_heads * (head_dim * 2);
+    int stride_kvh = head_dim * 2;
+
+    float q_val = __bfloat162float(
+        query[batch_idx * num_heads * head_dim + head_idx * head_dim + tid]);
+
+    const int* bt = block_table + batch_idx * max_blocks;
+
+    for (int pg = 0; pg < num_pages; ++pg) {
+        int blk_id = bt[pg];
+        int tok_this = (pg == num_pages - 1) ? (seq_len - pg * page_size) : page_size;
+
+        int base = blk_id * stride_blk + kv_head * stride_kvh;
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < page_size; ++pos) {
+            int off = base + pos * stride_pos;
+            if (pos < tok_this)
+                k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = kv_cache[off + tid];
+            else
+                k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid] = __float2bfloat16(0.0f);
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        float partial[16];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < page_size; ++pos)
+            partial[pos] = q_val * __bfloat162float(k_smem[pos * head_dim + tid]);
+
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int pos = 0; pos < page_size; ++pos) {
+            float val = partial[pos];
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1)
+                val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, off);
+            if (lane == 0) scratch[pos * num_warps + warp_id] = val;
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        if (tid < tok_this) {
+            float score = 0.0f;
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int w = 0; w < num_warps; ++w)
+                score += scratch[tid * num_warps + w];
+            scores_out[pg * page_size + tid] = score * scale;
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+    }
+}
+
+
+extern "C" int launch_debug_scores(
+    void* q, void* kv, void* bt, void* sl, void* out,
+    int bi, int hi, int H, int Hkv, int D, int P, int mb,
+    float scale, cudaStream_t stream)
+{
+    int L = 1024;  // hardcoded for debug
+    int grid = 1, block = D;
+    int num_warps = (D + WARP_SIZE - 1) / WARP_SIZE;
+    int smem = (int)(P * D * 2 + P * num_warps * 4);
+
+    cudaGetLastError();
+    debug_scores_kernel<<<grid, block, smem, stream>>>(
+        (const __nv_bfloat16*)q, (const __nv_bfloat16*)kv,
+        (const int*)bt, (const int*)sl, (float*)out,
+        bi, hi, H, Hkv, D, P, mb, scale);
+    return (int)cudaGetLastError();
+}

import reference

lib = ctypes.CDLL('./kernel_debug.so')
lib.launch_debug_scores.argtypes = [ctypes.c_void_p]*5 + [ctypes.c_int]*6 + [ctypes.c_float, ctypes.c_void_p]
lib.launch_debug_scores.restype = ctypes.c_int

reference.BATCH=8; reference.NUM_HEADS=32; reference.NUM_KV_HEADS=8
reference.HEAD_DIM=128; reference.SEQ_LEN=1024; reference.PAGE_SIZE=16
torch.manual_seed(123)
inputs = [t for t in reference.get_inputs()]

b=2; h=27; D=128; L=1024; P=16; Hkv=8; kvh=6
scale = 1.0/math.sqrt(D)

# CUDA kernel scores
scores_gpu = torch.zeros(L, dtype=torch.float32, device='cuda')
q_gpu = (inputs[0].cuda() * 8.0)
kv_gpu = (inputs[1].cuda() * 8.0)
bt_gpu = inputs[2].cuda()
sl_gpu = inputs[3].cuda()

err = lib.launch_debug_scores(
    ctypes.c_void_p(q_gpu.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(kv_gpu.data_ptr()),
    ctypes.c_void_p(bt_gpu.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(sl_gpu.data_ptr()),
    ctypes.c_void_p(scores_gpu.data_ptr()),
    b, h, 32, 8, D, P, inputs[2].shape[1],
    ctypes.c_float(scale), ctypes.c_void_p(0))
torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Reference scores
kv = kv_gpu.cpu().index_select(0, bt_gpu[b][:64].long())
kv = kv.reshape(64*P, Hkv, 2*D)[:L]

import torch, math, numpy as np, sys; sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import reference

# Simulate the EXACT online softmax algorithm from the kernel, step by step
reference.BATCH=8; reference.NUM_HEADS=32; reference.NUM_KV_HEADS=8
reference.HEAD_DIM=128; reference.SEQ_LEN=1024; reference.PAGE_SIZE=16
torch.manual_seed(123)
inputs = [t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
# large_q_kv scaling
inputs = [inputs[0]*8.0, inputs[1]*8.0, inputs[2], inputs[3]]

b=2; h=27; D=128; L=1024; P=16; Hkv=8; kvh=6
scale = 1.0/math.sqrt(D)

# Gather KV like the reference
bt = inputs[2][b]
num_pages = (L+P-1)//P
pages = bt[:num_pages].long()
kv = inputs[1].index_select(0,pages).reshape(num_pages*P, Hkv, 2*D)[:L]
k_all = kv[:,kvh,:D].float()
v_all = kv[:,kvh,D:].float()
q = inputs[0][b,h].float()

# Reference: batch softmax
scores_ref = (q @ k_all.T) * scale
probs_ref = torch.softmax(scores_ref, dim=-1)
out_ref = (probs_ref.unsqueeze(0) @ v_all.unsqueeze(0).transpose(1,2)?)
# Actually: out_ref shape (D,) = sum_l probs[l] * v[l,:]
out_ref = torch.einsum('l,ld->d', probs_ref, v_all)

# Kernel simulation: online softmax with fp64 running state
running_max = -1e30
running_sum = 0.0
acc = np.zeros(D, dtype=np.float64)

for pg in range(num_pages):
    start = pg * P
    end = min(start + P, L)
    tok_this = end - start
    
    scores_page = scores_ref[start:end].numpy().astype(np.float32)
    v_page = v_all[start:end].numpy().astype(np.float32)
    
    page_max_f = float(scores_page.max())
    new_max = max(running_max, page_max_f)
    rescale = math.exp(running_max - new_max)
    running_sum = running_sum * rescale
    acc = acc * rescale
    
    # exp(scores - new_max) in float32
    exp_scores = np.exp(scores_page - np.float32(new_max)).astype(np.float64)
    running_sum += exp_scores.sum()
    
    for t in range(tok_this):
        acc += exp_scores[t] * v_page[t].astype(np.float64)

    running_max = new_max

out_kernel_sim = acc / running_sum  # fp64

diff = np.abs(out_ref.numpy().astype(np.float64) - out_kernel_sim)
print(f'Online softmax sim (fp64 running state): max_diff={diff.max():.12f}')

# Also try with fp32 running state
running_max_f = -1e30
running_sum_f = 0.0
acc_f = np.zeros(D, dtype=np.float32)
for pg in range(num_pages):
    start = pg * P; end = min(start+P, L); tok_this = end-start
    scores_page = scores_ref[start:end].numpy().astype(np.float32)
    v_page = v_all[start:end].numpy().astype(np.float32)
    page_max_f = float(scores_page.max())
    new_max = max(running_max_f, page_max_f)
    rescale = np.float32(math.exp(running_max_f - new_max))
    running_sum_f = running_sum_f * rescale
    acc_f = acc_f * rescale
    exp_scores = np.exp(scores_page - np.float32(new_max)).astype(np.float32)
    running_sum_f += exp_scores.sum()
    for t in range(tok_this):
        acc_f += exp_scores[t] * v_page[t]
    running_max_f = new_max

out_kernel_sim_f32 = acc_f / running_sum_f

The total throughput per SM is (2 heads / time) for non-fused vs (32 heads / time) for fused. reasoning (2410 chars)
This is WORSE! The GQA-fused kernel is much slower (22ms instead of 5ms for shape 1). The issue is that with `grid = B * Hkv`, we have much fewer blocks. For shape 1 (B=32, Hkv=8): 256 blocks. But each block now processes 4 heads sequentially, which takes 4x longer. And with only 64 blocks (B*Hkv for this shape), only 64/82 ≈ 78% of SMs are used. Wait, shape 1 has B=32, Hkv=8, so grid = 256. Each block processes 4 heads (since H/Hkv=4). Each block takes ~4x the work. With the 2-pass approach, each block does 2 passes over KV. In the non-fused version: grid = B*H = 32*32 = 1024, each block processes 1 head with 2 passes. In the fused version: grid = B*Hkv = 32*8 = 256, each block processes 4 heads with 2 passes per head (but K loaded once). The fused version has 4x fewer blocks, each doing 4x more work. Total work is the same, but occupancy suffers because of fewer blocks and larger shared memory. The shared memory for MAX_GROUP=4, MAX_SEQ_LEN=4096: scores = 4*4096*4 = 64KB. Plus K+V = 8KB. Total = 72KB. Each block uses 72KB, so only 1 block per SM (100KB max, but only 1 fits due to 72KB each). With 1 block per SM and 82 SMs, only 82 blocks run concurrently. With 256 total blocks, that's 3 waves. In the non-fused version: smem = 20KB. 2 blocks per SM. 164 concurrent. With 1024 total blocks, that's 6 waves. Each wave is faster (1/4 the work). Net result: fused version takes 23ms vs 5ms for non-fused. BAD. The issue is that MAX_SEQ_LEN=4096 makes scores take 64KB for 4 heads. I should reduce MAX_SEQ_LEN to the actual seq_len. But the smem is allocated at compile time (MAX_SEQ_LEN is a constant). Alternatively, I could make MAX_SEQ_LEN smaller and only support shorter sequences with fusion, falling back to non-fused for longer ones. But the real issue is that the fused kernel has too much shared memory, limiting occupancy to 1 block per SM. Let me go back to the non-fused approach. It's slower than desired (0.038 peak) but it's correct. At this point, I have a working, correct kernel that passes all tests. The performance is below the 0.1 threshold for OK. Given the constraints (stride-based KV cache layout, RTX 3090 memory subsystem), reaching 0.1 would require significant optimization that I don't have time for in this session. Let me clean up the solution, remove debug files, make sure the final solution.py loads the correct kernel.so, and document findings.
